{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01892zU5KU6eiyJKE6oUBkXx","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Now I need to understand what's happening when the RPC task receives ERESTARTSYS from `out_of_line_wait_on_bit`. Looking at `rpc_wait_bit_killable`:\n\n```c\nstatic int rpc_wait_bit_killable(struct wait_bit_key *key)\n{\n    if (fatal_signal_pending(current))\n        return -ERESTARTSYS;\n    freezable_schedule_unsafe();\n    return 0;\n}\n```\n\nThis returns -ERESTARTSYS if there's a fatal signal pending. But the bug says the server does reply. The issue must be that between when the task goes to sleep and when it wakes up for the reply, a signal arrives and causes it to return -ERESTARTSYS even though the reply arrived.\n\n\nThe loop in `__rpc_execute` should go around once more after getting ERESTARTSYS to catch any callbacks, so if the reply arrived it should be processed in that next iteration. But maybe the reply processing is asynchronous and hasn't been matched to the task yet by the time the signal causes an early exit, or the task's state gets reset before the reply can be properly handled. This feels like a timing issue where the signal interrupts the flow before the reply can be fully integrated.\n\nLooking at this as a regression between those kernel versions, something changed in how the RPC client or NFS handles signals and replies. The core problem is clear: the server responds correctly but the client never sees it and returns ERESTARTSYS instead. This points to the signal being raised before the reply arrives, or possibly something in the autofs unmount path that's sending a signal to the process and breaking its NFS RPC calls. Though `rpc_wait_bit_killable` only returns ERESTARTSYS on fatal signals, so a non-fatal autofs signal would just reschedule. I need to understand how NFS actually handles the case where an RPC completes...\n\nWhen the signal arrives at the same time as the reply, the `__rpc_execute` loop marks the task as killed and exits with ERESTARTSYS. But if the reply already arrived before the signal, the reply processing would have set the task status and woken it up. The timing here is critical—I need to trace through `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` to see exactly when the bit gets cleared and how that interacts with signal delivery. The function should return 0 if the bit gets cleared, regardless of any pending signals. Let me trace through how `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` actually works: it checks if the bit is already clear and returns immediately, otherwise it invokes the action function which handles signal checking and scheduling. So when a reply clears the `RPC_TASK_QUEUED` bit and wakes the task, `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` should see the cleared bit and return success without ever calling the action function.\n\nBut there's another scenario: if a fatal signal arrives before the reply, `rpc_wait_bit_killable` returns -ERESTARTSYS and the task gets marked as killed. The real issue might be that a reply arrives after the signal has already interrupted the wait, leaving the RPC client in an inconsistent state where the reply doesn't get properly matched to the task. This is a known race condition in NFS, so I need to look at how the transport layer handles replies for tasks that have been {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_014cqngk6fouPp8KBJEsEt8L","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Actually, let me think about this from the perspective of a known bug fix. The bug report mentions kernel 4.1.15, which is a fairly specific version. The bug was introduced between 3.14.12 and 4.1.15. Let me think about what could cause ERESTARTSYS from an NFS getattr request.\n\nThe key clue is: \"tcpdump shows, that server replies to getattr without error, but client doesn't see this reply, and returning error instead.\"\n\nThis could mean the reply arrives but is discarded or not processed. This could be due to:\n1. The socket being closed/reset before the reply is processed\n\n\n2. A race condition in the reply handling path\n\nThe -512 error (ERESTARTSYS) points to signal interruption, which makes sense given that autofs sends signals during mount expiry. But the real question is what changed between 3.14.12 and 4.1.15 that made the NFS code vulnerable to this signal timing issue, particularly in how errors are propagated from the RPC layer back through the lookup revalidation path.\n\nLooking at the error handling in `nfs_lookup_revalidate`, when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` calls down to `__nfs_revalidate_inode` and gets -ERESTARTSYS back, that error code bubbles up through the call stack. The question is whether the VFS layer is properly handling this negative return value as an error condition versus treating it as an invalid cache entry. So `out_bad` returns 0 to invalidate the dentry, while `out_error` would return the actual error code—when ERESTARTSYS comes back from `__nfs_revalidate_inode`, it takes the `out_bad` path and invalidates the dentry, which for autofs mounts causes the mount to disappear. I'm wondering if this behavior changed between kernel versions, or if the real issue is upstream in how the RPC layer handles the signal.\n\nLooking at the `nfs_lookup_revalidate` function, when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns non-zero (including ERESTARTSYS), it goes straight to `out_zap_parent` and invalidates the dentry. But maybe ERESTARTSYS should be propagated as an error instead of being treated as a fatal validation failure.\n\nActually, I'm starting to think the real issue might be in `__rpc_execute` itself. When a signal kills the task with ERESTARTSYS, the RPC reply could arrive at the network level just after the task exits, leaving the reply unprocessed and effectively lost. This could be a request slot or XID reuse problem—if the task exits early but the request is still pending in the transport, a subsequent request might collide with the old reply.\n\nLooking at `nfs_lookup_revalidate`, I'm noticing that when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns an error, it goes to `out_zap_parent` which falls through to `out_bad` and returns 0, marking the dentry invalid. If that error was ERESTARTSYS, the dentry gets invalidated unnecessarily instead of being retried. I need to trace through the VFS dentry revalidation logic to understand what happens when `d_revalidate` returns 0 versus a negative error code.\n\nThe issue is becoming clearer: if `nfs_lookup_revalidate` returns 0 (invalid), the VFS drops the dentry entirely, but if it returned the actual error code like -ERESTARTSYS, the VFS might handle it differently and allow a retry instead of removing the autofs mount point.\n\nLooking at the code path, when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns an error, the current logic goes through `out_zap_parent` to `out_bad` which returns 0, invalidating the dentry. But there's an `out_error` label that returns the actual error code—the fix might be to route error cases there instead so the error propagates up rather than silently invalidating the dentry. I need to look into how the kernel handles this distinction between ENOENT (which should invalidate the dentry) and transient errors like ERESTARTSYS (which should propagate as errors instead). If a signal arrives during the wait, `rpc_call_sync` returns -ERESTARTSYS.\n\nThen:1. `__nfs_revalidate_inode` returns -ERESTARTSYS\n2. `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns -ERESTARTSYS (since ret != 0 at line 1074-1075)\n\n\n3. Back in `nfs_lookup_revalidate`, when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns an error, the code checks if it's in RCU mode and either returns -ECHILD or goes to `out_zap_parent`, which eventually invalidates the dentry and returns 0.\n4. This causes the VFS to remove the dentry, making the autofs mount disappear.\n5. The fix needs to distinguish between errors that should invalidate the dentry versus signal interruptions that should be retried.\n\nLooking at the code paths, `out_bad` returns 0 to invalidate the dentry, while `out_error` propagates the actual error code back to the caller. For errors like -ERESTARTSYS, we should use `out_error` instead of `out_zap_parent` so the error gets propagated rather than treating it as a stale inode. The bug is in `fs/nfs/dir.c` in the `nfs_lookup_revalidate` function where it needs to check the error type before deciding whether to invalidate or propagate.\n\nNow I'm also noticing the lookup operation itself has the same issue—when the NFS protocol lookup fails with an error, it goes to `out_bad` instead of checking if it should go to `out_error` for certain error codes like -ERESTARTSYS. The `out_error` path properly cleans up the allocated resources and propagates the error, so that's where restartable errors should go. I need to trace through the actual execution path in nfs_lookup_revalidate for the /storage dentry to understand where it's going wrong. The verifier check probably fails due to recent activity, then it proceeds through the validation steps—the inode isn't stale, but somewhere around the NFS protocol lookup call is where the error handling diverges between versions, likely causing ERESTARTSYS to incorrectly return invalid instead of propagating the error.\n\nActually, looking at the debug output more carefully, the real issue is that nfs_check_verifier is passing, so it's proceeding to nfs_lookup_verify_inode which calls __nfs_revalidate_inode, and that's where the getattr RPC is failing with -512 (ERESTARTSYS). The signal is interrupting the revalidation process and the error handling is treating it as a fatal validation failure rather than a restartable condition. I'm reconsidering whether this is the right function to fix. Looking back at `nfs_lookup_verify_inode`, it seems the issue is that when it encounters a transient error like -ERESTARTSYS, the code should return that error directly rather than treating it as a stale inode condition that requires invalidation. I need to figure out where exactly this bug lives in the codebase. It's definitely in `fs/nfs/dir.c` within `nfs_lookup_revalidate` — the issue is that when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns an error like -ERESTARTSYS, the code incorrectly treats it as a stale dentry and goes to `out_zap_parent` instead of properly propagating the error. Let me also consider whether the IS_AUTOMOUNT check in `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` plays a role here.\n\nLooking at the scenario more carefully: /storage is a directory on the NFS root filesystem with autofs indirect mounts underneath it. When the autofs entry expires and `nfs_lookup_revalidate` gets called for the /storage dentry itself, the issue becomes clearer — /storage is on NFS root, not the autofs mount point, so the IS_AUTOMOUNT check wouldn't short-circuit the validation.\n\nThe debug output points directly to the problem: getattr fails with -512 (ERESTARTSYS), causing `nfs_revalidate_inode` to fail, which then makes `nfs_lookup_revalidate` mark /storage as invalid. The bug is likely in how `nfs_lookup_revalidate` handles this revalidation failure.\n\nLooking at the code path, when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns an error, it goes to `out_zap_parent` which clears the parent directory's caches and then falls through to `out_bad`, returning an error. The issue is that this invalidates the dentry when it should probably be handling the transient error differently—a -512 error is a signal to restart the syscall, not necessarily that the dentry is bad. I need to trace back through the RPC layer to understand where the -512 error originates—it's coming from `rpc_call_sync` in the getattr call, which means a signal interrupted the operation. The real question is whether the bug is in how `nfs_lookup_revalidate` handles transient errors versus permanent failures, or if it's deeper in the RPC handling itself. I'm wondering if a fatal signal gets sent to the process during the umount operation—maybe automountd or the autofs driver itself triggers one. Looking at the tcpdump evidence more carefully, it seems the server actually replied successfully to the getattr request, but the client never processed it, which suggests the task was already being killed when the response arrived. The core issue is that `rpc_wait_bit_killable` returns ERESTARTSYS specifically when a fatal signal is pending, so something is delivering a fatal signal (SIGKILL, SIGSTOP, or a termination signal) to what should be a stable daemon or kernel thread during the mount operation.\n\nOn a diskless machine with root on NFS, the getattr is happening in-kernel when a user process calls something like `stat(/storage)`, triggering an NFS RPC call synchronously in that process's context. If that process receives SIGKILL while waiting for the RPC reply, the call fails with ERESTARTSYS—but that raises the question of why SIGKILL would be sent to an innocent process just doing a stat call. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01WNuFXRyhT9xecxzeC1sGFs","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"So autofs4 defines `SHUTDOWN_SIGS = sigmask(SIGKILL) | sigmask(SIGINT) | sigmask(SIGQUIT)`. These are signals that can interrupt a pending mount.\n\nNow, `SIGKILL` is a fatal signal. Looking at `rpc_wait_bit_killable`:\n```c\nstatic int rpc_wait_bit_killable(struct wait_bit_key *key){\n    if (fatal_signal_pending(current))        return -ERESTARTSYS;\n    freezable_schedule_unsafe();\n    return 0;}\n```\n\n`fatal_signal_pending` returns true for SIGKILL.\n\n\nLet me trace through what actually happens during the expiry and unmount sequence. When autofs triggers expiration, it notifies automountd to unmount the volume, which calls `umount()`. During that unmount operation, a dentry revalidation of /storage gets triggered, and that revalidation attempts an NFS get—which is where the hang or error occurs.\n\nThe getattr fails with ERESTARTSYS because there's a pending signal on the process handling the revalidation. The question is what's causing that signal in the first place—is the kernel's umount code sending it, or did something change in the sunrpc layer between kernel versions 3.14 and 4.1 that made it more prone to returning ERESTARTSYS? When `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` or the `lookup` call returns an error, the code jumps to `out_bad` which invalidates the dentry instead of propagating the actual error through `out_error`.
